<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Dmitri Mendeleev</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#searchInput">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-body-content mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Russian chemist (1834–1907)</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">"Mendeleev" redirects here. For other uses, see <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Mendeleyev_(disambiguation)" title="Mendeleyev (disambiguation)">Mendeleyev (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">In this name that follows <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Slavic_naming_customs" title="Eastern Slavic naming customs">Eastern Slavic naming conventions</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Patronymic" title="Patronymic">patronymic</a> is <i> Ivanovich</i> and the <a href="/wiki/Surname" title="Surname">family name</a> is <i> Mendeleev</i>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p><b>Dmitri Ivanovich Mendeleev</b> (sometimes transliterated as <b>Mendeleyev</b> or <b>Mendeleef</b>) (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><small>English: </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɛ/: 'e' in 'dress'">ɛ</span><span title="'n' in 'nigh'">n</span><span title="'d' in 'dye'">d</span><span title="/əl/: 'le' in 'bottle'">əl</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="/ə/: 'a' in 'about'">ə</span><span title="'f' in 'find'">f</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">MEN</span>-dəl-<span style="font-size:90%">AY</span>-əf</i></a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> Russian: <span lang="ru">Дмитрий Иванович Менделеев</span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[a]</a></sup> <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Russian" title="Romanization of Russian">tr.</a></small> <span title="Russian-language text"><i lang="ru-Latn">Dmitriy Ivanovich Mendeleyev</i></span>, <small>IPA: </small><span class="IPA" lang="ru-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Russian" title="Help:IPA/Russian">[ˈdmʲitrʲɪj ɪˈvanəvʲɪtɕ mʲɪnʲdʲɪˈlʲejɪf]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ru-Dmitri_Mendeleev.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/61/Ru-Dmitri_Mendeleev.ogg" title="Ru-Dmitri Mendeleev.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>; 8 February [<a href="/wiki/Old_Style_and_New_Style_dates" title="Old Style and New Style dates">O.S.</a> 27 January] 1834 – 2 February [O.S. 20 January] 1907) was a Russian <a href="/wiki/Chemist" title="Chemist">chemist</a> and inventor. He is best known for formulating the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Periodic_Law" title="Periodic Law">Periodic Law</a> and creating a version of the <a href="/wiki/Periodic_table" title="Periodic table">periodic table of elements</a>. He used the Periodic Law not only to correct the then-accepted properties of some known elements, such as the valence and atomic weight of uranium, but also to predict the properties of three elements that were yet to be discovered.
</p>

style="width:277px;max-width:277px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:137px;max-width:137px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:188px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MendeleevaMD.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2004" data-file-width="1430" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/MendeleevaMD.jpg/135px-MendeleevaMD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/MendeleevaMD.jpg/203px-MendeleevaMD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/MendeleevaMD.jpg/270px-MendeleevaMD.jpg 2x" width="135"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:136px;max-width:136px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:188px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MendeleevIP.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2004" data-file-width="1424" decoding="async" height="189" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/MendeleevIP.jpg/134px-MendeleevIP.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/MendeleevIP.jpg/201px-MendeleevIP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/MendeleevIP.jpg/268px-MendeleevIP.jpg 2x" width="134"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Portraits of Maria Dmitrievna Mendeleeva and Ivan Pavlovich Mendeleev (c. early 19th century)</div></div></div></div>
<p>Mendeleev was born in the village of Verkhnie Aremzyani, near <a href="/wiki/Tobolsk" title="Tobolsk">Tobolsk</a> in <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Ivan_Pavlovich_Mendeleev&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Ivan Pavlovich Mendeleev (page does not exist)">Ivan Pavlovich Mendeleev</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size:85%; font-style: normal;"> [<a class="extiw" href="https://ru.wikipedia.org/wiki/%D0%9C%D0%B5%D0%BD%D0%B4%D0%B5%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B5%D0%B2,_%D0%98%D0%B2%D0%B0%D0%BD_%D0%9F%D0%B0%D0%B2%D0%BB%D0%BE%D0%B2%D0%B8%D1%87" title="ru:Менделеев, Иван Павлович">ru</a>]</span> (1783–1847) and Maria Dmitrievna Mendeleeva (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/N%C3%A9e" title="Née">née</a> Kornilieva) (1793–1850).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-notes_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-notes-5">[4]</a></sup> Ivan worked as a school principal and a teacher of fine arts, politics and philosophy at the <a href="/wiki/Tambov" title="Tambov">Tambov</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saratov" title="Saratov">Saratov</a> gymnasiums.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pavel_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-pavel-6">[5]</a></sup> Ivan's father, Pavel Maximovich Sokolov, was a <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox</a> priest from the <a href="/wiki/Tver" title="Tver">Tver</a> region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[6]</a></sup> As per the tradition of priests of that time, Pavel's children were given new family names while attending the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theological_seminary" title="Theological seminary">theological seminary</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup> with Ivan getting the family name Mendeleev after the name of a local landlord.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9">[8]</a></sup>
</p><p>Maria Kornilieva came from a well-known family of Tobolsk merchants, founders of the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siberian" title="Siberian">Siberian</a> printing house who traced their ancestry to Yakov Korniliev, a 17th-century <a href="/wiki/Posad" title="Posad">posad</a> man turned a wealthy merchant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-int_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-int-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> In 1889, a local librarian published an article in the Tobolsk newspaper where he claimed that Yakov was a baptized <a href="/wiki/Teleuts" title="Teleuts">Teleut</a>, an ethnic minority known as "white <a href="/wiki/Kalmyks" title="Kalmyks">Kalmyks</a>" at the time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> Since no sources were provided and no documented facts of Yakov's life were ever revealed, biographers generally dismiss it as a myth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[13]</a></sup> In 1908, shortly after Mendeleev's death, one of his nieces published <i>Family Chronicles. Memories about D. I. Mendeleev</i> where she voiced "a family legend" about Maria's grandfather who married "a <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyz_people" title="Kyrgyz people">Kyrgyz</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tatar" title="Tatar">Tatar</a> beauty whom he loved so much that when she died, he also died from grief".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> This, however, contradicts the documented family chronicles, and neither of those legends is supported by Mendeleev's autobiography, his daughter's or his wife's memoirs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-notes_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-notes-5">[4]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup> Yet some Western scholars still refer to Mendeleev's supposed "Mongol", "Tatar", "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tartaria" title="Tartaria">Tartarian</a>" or simply "Asian" ancestry as a fact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[20]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mendeleev was raised as an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox" title="Eastern Orthodox">Orthodox Christian</a>, his mother encouraging him to "patiently search divine and scientific truth".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[21]</a></sup> His son would later inform her that he departed from the Church and embraced a form of "romanticized <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">deism</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gordin_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gordin-23">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mendeleev was the youngest of 17 siblings, of whom "only 14 stayed alive to be baptized" according to Mendeleev's brother Pavel, meaning the others died soon after their birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pavel_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-pavel-6">[5]</a></sup> The exact number of Mendeleev's siblings differs among sources and is still a matter of some historical dispute.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[b]</a></sup> Unfortunately for the family's financial well-being, his father became blind and lost his teaching position. His mother was forced to work and she restarted her family's abandoned glass factory. At the age of 13, after the passing of his father and the destruction of his mother's factory by fire, Mendeleev attended the <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">Gymnasium</a> in Tobolsk.
</p><p>In 1849, his mother took Mendeleev across Russia from Siberia to Moscow with the aim of getting Mendeleev enrolled at the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_State_University" title="Moscow State University">Moscow University</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-9">[8]</a></sup> The university in Moscow did not accept him. The mother and son continued to <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a> to the father's alma mater. The now poor Mendeleev family relocated to Saint Petersburg, where he entered the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Main_Pedagogical_Institute" title="Main Pedagogical Institute">Main Pedagogical Institute</a> in 1850. After graduation, he contracted <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, causing him to move to the <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimean</a> Peninsula on the northern coast of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> in 1855. While there, he became a science master of the <a href="/wiki/Simferopol_gymnasium_%E2%84%961" title="Simferopol gymnasium №1">1st Simferopol Gymnasium</a>. In 1857, he returned to Saint Petersburg with fully restored health.
</p><p>Between 1859 and 1861, he worked on the <a href="/wiki/Capillary_action" title="Capillary action">capillarity of liquids</a> and the workings of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spectroscope" title="Spectroscope">spectroscope</a> in <a href="/wiki/Heidelberg" title="Heidelberg">Heidelberg</a>. Later in 1861, he published a textbook named <i>Organic Chemistry</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2003509_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2003509-28">[26]</a></sup> This won him the Demidov Prize of the Petersburg Academy of Sciences.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2003509_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2003509-28">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 4 April 1862, he became engaged to Feozva Nikitichna Leshcheva, and they married on 27 April 1862 at <a href="/wiki/Military_Engineering-Technical_University" title="Military Engineering-Technical University">Nikolaev Engineering Institute</a>'s church in Saint Petersburg (where he taught).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mendeleev became a professor at the <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg_State_Institute_of_Technology" title="Saint Petersburg State Institute of Technology">Saint Petersburg Technological Institute</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg_State_University" title="Saint Petersburg State University">Saint Petersburg State University</a> in 1864,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2003509_28-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2003509-28">[26]</a></sup> and 1865, respectively. In 1865, he became a Doctor of Science for his dissertation "On the Combinations of Water with Alcohol". He achieved <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tenure" title="Tenure">tenure</a> in 1867 at St. Petersburg University and started to teach inorganic chemistry while succeeding Voskresenskii to this post;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2003509_28-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2003509-28">[26]</a></sup> by 1871, he had transformed Saint Petersburg into an internationally recognized center for chemistry research.
</p>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content">

<li><b> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Chemistry" title="Portal:Chemistry">Chemistry Portal</a></b></li></ul></td>
</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Sidebar_periodic_table" title="Template:Sidebar periodic table"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Sidebar_periodic_table" title="Template talk:Sidebar periodic table"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Sidebar_periodic_table&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Periodic_table_monument.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Periodic_table_monument.jpg/220px-Periodic_table_monument.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Periodic_table_monument.jpg/330px-Periodic_table_monument.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Periodic_table_monument.jpg/440px-Periodic_table_monument.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Periodic_table_monument.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sculpture in honor of Mendeleev and the periodic table, located in <a href="/wiki/Bratislava" title="Bratislava">Bratislava</a>, Slovakia</div></div></div>
<p>In 1863, there were 56 known <a href="/wiki/Chemical_element" title="Chemical element">elements</a> with a new element being discovered at a rate of approximately one per year. Other scientists had previously identified periodicity of elements. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/John_Alexander_Reina_Newlands" title="John Alexander Reina Newlands">John Newlands</a> described a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Law_of_Octaves" title="Law of Octaves">Law of Octaves</a>, noting their periodicity according to relative atomic weight in 1864, publishing it in 1865. His proposal identified the potential for new elements such as <a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>. The concept was criticized, and his innovation was not recognized by the Society of Chemists until 1887. Another person to propose a periodic table was <a href="/wiki/Lothar_Meyer" title="Lothar Meyer">Lothar Meyer</a>, who published a paper in 1864 describing 28 elements classified by their valence, but with no predictions of new elements.
</p><p>After becoming a teacher in 1867, Mendeleev wrote <i>Principles of Chemistry</i> (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru">Основы химии</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Russian" title="Romanization of Russian">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Russian-language romanization"><i lang="ru-Latn">Osnovy khimii</i></span>), which became the definitive textbook of its time. It was published in two volumes between 1868 and 1870, and Mendeleev wrote it as he was preparing a textbook for his course.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2003509_28-4"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2003509-28">[26]</a></sup> This is when he made his most important discovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2003509_28-5"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeilbron2003509-28">[26]</a></sup> As he attempted to classify the elements according to their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chemical" title="Chemical">chemical</a> properties, he noticed patterns that led him to postulate his periodic table; he claimed to have envisioned the complete arrangement of the elements in a <a href="/wiki/Dream" title="Dream">dream</a>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[32]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>I saw in a dream a table where all elements fell into place as required. Awakening, I immediately wrote it down on a piece of paper, only in one place did a correction later seem necessary.</p><div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Mendeleev, as quoted by Inostrantzev<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[34]</a></sup></cite></div></blockquote>
<p>Unaware of the earlier work on periodic tables going on in the 1860s, he made the following table:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tbody><tr>
<td>Cl 35.5</td>
<td>K 39</td>
<td>Ca 40
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>Br 80</td>
<td>Rb 85</td>
<td>Sr 88
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td>I 127</td>
<td>Cs 133</td>
<td>Ba 137
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>By adding additional elements following this pattern, Mendeleev developed his extended version of the periodic table.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[36]</a></sup> On 6 March 1869, he made a formal presentation to the Russian Chemical Society, titled <i>The Dependence between the Properties of the Atomic Weights of the Elements</i>, which described elements according to both <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_weight" title="Atomic weight">atomic weight</a> (now called <i>relative atomic mass</i>) and <a href="/wiki/Valence_(chemistry)" title="Valence (chemistry)">valence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Seaborg1994_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Seaborg1994-39">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pfennig2015_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pfennig2015-40">[38]</a></sup> This presentation stated that
</p>
<ol><li>The elements, if arranged according to their atomic weight, exhibit an apparent periodicity of properties.</li>
<li>Elements which are similar regarding their chemical properties either have similar atomic weights (e.g., Pt, Ir, Os) or have their atomic weights increasing regularly (e.g., K, Rb, Cs).</li>
<li>The arrangement of the elements in groups of elements in the order of their atomic weights corresponds to their so-called valencies, as well as, to some extent, to their distinctive chemical properties; as is apparent among other series in that of Li, Be, B, C, N, O, and F.</li>
<li>The elements which are the most widely diffused have small atomic weights.</li>
<li>The magnitude of the atomic weight determines the character of the element, just as the magnitude of the molecule determines the character of a compound body.</li>
<li>We must expect the discovery of many yet unknown elements – for example, two elements, analogous to aluminium and <a href="/wiki/Silicon" title="Silicon">silicon</a>, whose atomic weights would be between 65 and 75.</li>
<li>The atomic weight of an element may sometimes be amended by a knowledge of those of its contiguous elements. Thus the atomic weight of <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">tellurium</a> must lie between 123 and 126, and cannot be 128. (Tellurium's atomic weight is 127.6, and Mendeleev was incorrect in his assumption that atomic weight must increase with position within a period.)</li>
<li>Certain characteristic properties of elements can be foretold from their atomic weights.</li></ol>
<p>Mendeleev published his periodic table of all known elements and predicted several new elements to complete the table in a Russian-language journal. Only a few months after, Meyer published a virtually identical table in a German-language journal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nye_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nye-41">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Babel_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Babel-42">[40]</a></sup> Mendeleev has the distinction of accurately predicting the properties of what he called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dmitri_Mendeleev%27s_predicted_elements" title="Dmitri Mendeleev's predicted elements">ekasilicon, ekaaluminium and ekaboron</a> (<a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scandium" title="Scandium">scandium</a>, respectively).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marshall_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Marshall-43">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weeks_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weeks-44">[42]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mendeleev also proposed changes in the properties of some known elements. Prior to his work, <a href="/wiki/Uranium" title="Uranium">uranium</a> was supposed to have valence 3 and atomic weight about 120. Mendeleev realized that these values did not fit in his periodic table, and doubled both to valence 6 and atomic weight 240 (close to the modern value of 238).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>For his predicted three elements, he used the prefixes of eka, dvi, and tri (<a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> one, two, three) in their naming. Mendeleev questioned some of the currently accepted atomic weights (they could be measured only with a relatively low accuracy at that time), pointing out that they did not correspond to those suggested by his Periodic Law. He noted that <a href="/wiki/Tellurium" title="Tellurium">tellurium</a> has a higher atomic weight than <a href="/wiki/Iodine" title="Iodine">iodine</a>, but he placed them in the right order, incorrectly predicting that the accepted atomic weights at the time were at fault. He was puzzled about where to put the known <a href="/wiki/Lanthanide" title="Lanthanide">lanthanides</a>, and predicted the existence of another row to the table which were the <a href="/wiki/Actinide" title="Actinide">actinides</a> which were some of the heaviest in atomic weight. Some people dismissed Mendeleev for predicting that there would be more elements, but he was proven to be correct when Ga (<a href="/wiki/Gallium" title="Gallium">gallium</a>) and Ge (<a href="/wiki/Germanium" title="Germanium">germanium</a>) were found in 1875 and 1886 respectively, fitting perfectly into the two missing spaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>By using Sanskrit prefixes to name "missing" elements, Mendeleev may have recorded his debt to the Sanskrit grammarians of ancient India, who had created theories of language based on their discovery of the two-dimensional patterns of speech sounds (exemplified by the <a href="/wiki/Shiva_Sutras" title="Shiva Sutras">Śivasūtras</a> in <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="Pāṇini">Pāṇini</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_grammar" title="Sanskrit grammar">Sanskrit grammar</a>). Mendeleev was a friend and colleague of the Sanskritist <a href="/wiki/Otto_von_B%C3%B6htlingk" title="Otto von Böhtlingk">Otto von Böhtlingk</a>, who was preparing the second edition of his book on <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="Pāṇini">Pāṇini</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[45]</a></sup> at about this time, and Mendeleev wished to honor <a href="/wiki/P%C4%81%E1%B9%87ini" title="Pāṇini">Pāṇini</a> with his nomenclature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>The original draft made by Mendeleev would be found years later and published under the name <i>Tentative System of Elements.</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p>Dmitri Mendeleev is often referred to as the Father of the Periodic Table. He called his table or matrix, "the Periodic System".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life">Later life</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dmitri_Mendeleev_1890s.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="608" data-file-width="478" decoding="async" height="280" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Dmitri_Mendeleev_1890s.jpg/220px-Dmitri_Mendeleev_1890s.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Dmitri_Mendeleev_1890s.jpg/330px-Dmitri_Mendeleev_1890s.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Dmitri_Mendeleev_1890s.jpg/440px-Dmitri_Mendeleev_1890s.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dmitri_Mendeleev_1890s.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dmitri Mendeleev, 1890s</div></div></div>
<p>In 1876, he became obsessed<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> with Anna Ivanova Popova and began courting her; in 1881 he proposed to her and threatened suicide if she refused. His divorce from Leshcheva was finalized one month after he had married Popova (on 2 April)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[51]</a></sup> in early 1882. Even after the divorce, Mendeleev was technically a <a href="/wiki/Bigamy" title="Bigamy">bigamist</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a> required at least seven years before lawful remarriage. His divorce and the surrounding controversy contributed to his failure to be admitted to the Russian Academy of Sciences (despite his international fame by that time). His daughter from his second marriage, Lyubov, became the wife of the famous Russian poet <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Blok" title="Alexander Blok">Alexander Blok</a>. His other children were son Vladimir (a sailor, he took part in the notable <a href="/wiki/Eastern_journey_of_Nicholas_II" title="Eastern journey of Nicholas II">Eastern journey of Nicholas II</a>) and daughter Olga, from his first marriage to Feozva, and son Ivan and twins from Anna.
</p><p>Though Mendeleev was widely honored by scientific organizations all over Europe, including (in 1882) the <a href="/wiki/Davy_Medal" title="Davy Medal">Davy Medal</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">Royal Society</a> of London (which later also awarded him the <a href="/wiki/Copley_Medal" title="Copley Medal">Copley Medal</a> in 1905),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-54">[52]</a></sup> he resigned from Saint Petersburg University on 17 August 1890. He was elected a <a href="/wiki/List_of_fellows_of_the_Royal_Society_elected_in_1892" title="List of fellows of the Royal Society elected in 1892">Foreign Member of the Royal Society (ForMemRS) in 1892</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-formemrs_1-1"><a href="#cite_note-formemrs-1">[1]</a></sup> and in 1893 he was appointed director of the Bureau of Weights and Measures, a post which he occupied until his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_54-1"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mendeleev also investigated the composition of petroleum, and helped to found the first <a href="/wiki/Oil_refinery" title="Oil refinery">oil refinery</a> in Russia. He recognized the importance of petroleum as a feedstock for <a href="/wiki/Petrochemical" title="Petrochemical">petrochemicals</a>. He is credited with a remark that burning petroleum as a fuel "would be akin to firing up a kitchen stove with bank notes".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:200_y_Anniversary_of_Berlin_Academy_1900.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1978" data-file-width="3097" decoding="async" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/200_y_Anniversary_of_Berlin_Academy_1900.jpg/220px-200_y_Anniversary_of_Berlin_Academy_1900.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/200_y_Anniversary_of_Berlin_Academy_1900.jpg/330px-200_y_Anniversary_of_Berlin_Academy_1900.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/200_y_Anniversary_of_Berlin_Academy_1900.jpg/440px-200_y_Anniversary_of_Berlin_Academy_1900.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:200_y_Anniversary_of_Berlin_Academy_1900.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mendeleev, <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Werner" title="Alfred Werner">Alfred Werner</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adolf_von_Baeyer" title="Adolf von Baeyer">Adolf von Baeyer</a>, and other prominent chemists</div></div></div>
<p>In 1905, Mendeleev was elected a member of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Swedish_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences">Royal Swedish Academy of Sciences</a>. The following year the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Committee_for_Chemistry" title="Nobel Committee for Chemistry">Nobel Committee for Chemistry</a> recommended to the Swedish Academy to award the <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Chemistry" title="Nobel Prize in Chemistry">Nobel Prize in Chemistry</a> for 1906 to Mendeleev for his discovery of the periodic system. The Chemistry Section of the Swedish Academy supported this recommendation. The Academy was then supposed to approve the Committee's choice, as it has done in almost every case. Unexpectedly, at the full meeting of the Academy, a dissenting member of the Nobel Committee, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Klason" title="Peter Klason">Peter Klason</a>, proposed the candidacy of <a href="/wiki/Henri_Moissan" title="Henri Moissan">Henri Moissan</a> whom he favored. <a href="/wiki/Svante_Arrhenius" title="Svante Arrhenius">Svante Arrhenius</a>, although not a member of the Nobel Committee for Chemistry, had a great deal of influence in the Academy and also pressed for the rejection of Mendeleev, arguing that the periodic system was too old to acknowledge its discovery in 1906. According to the contemporaries, Arrhenius was motivated by the grudge he held against Mendeleev for his critique of Arrhenius's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acid-base_reaction_theories#Arrhenius_definition" title="Acid-base reaction theories">dissociation theory</a>. After heated arguments, the majority of the Academy chose Moissan by a margin of one vote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[54]</a></sup> The attempts to nominate Mendeleev in 1907 were again frustrated by the absolute opposition of Arrhenius.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-7167-3103-7_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-isbn0-7167-3103-7-57">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1907, Mendeleev died at the age of 72 in <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a> from <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a>. His last words were to his physician: "Doctor, you have science, I have faith," which is possibly a <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne" title="Jules Verne">Jules Verne</a> quote.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_achievements">Other achievements</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kramskoy_Mendeleev_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3131" data-file-width="2244" decoding="async" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Kramskoy_Mendeleev_01.jpg/220px-Kramskoy_Mendeleev_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Kramskoy_Mendeleev_01.jpg/330px-Kramskoy_Mendeleev_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Kramskoy_Mendeleev_01.jpg/440px-Kramskoy_Mendeleev_01.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kramskoy_Mendeleev_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Dmitri Mendeleev by <a href="/wiki/Ivan_Kramskoi" title="Ivan Kramskoi">Ivan Kramskoi</a> (1878)</div></div></div>
<p>Mendeleev made other important contributions to chemistry. The Russian chemist and science historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lev_Aleksandrovich_Chugaev" title="Lev Aleksandrovich Chugaev">Lev Chugaev</a> characterized him as "a chemist of genius, first-class physicist, a fruitful researcher in the fields of hydrodynamics, meteorology, geology, certain branches of chemical technology (explosives, petroleum, and fuels, for example) and other disciplines adjacent to chemistry and physics, a thorough expert of chemical industry and industry in general, and an original thinker in the field of economy." Mendeleev was one of the founders, in 1869, of the Russian Chemical Society. He worked on the theory and practice of protectionist trade and on agriculture.
</p><p>In an attempt at a chemical conception of the <a href="/wiki/Aether_(classical_element)" title="Aether (classical element)">aether</a>, he put forward a hypothesis that there existed two inert chemical elements of lesser atomic weight than <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen" title="Hydrogen">hydrogen</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_54-2"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-54">[52]</a></sup> Of these two proposed elements, he thought the lighter to be an all-penetrating, all-pervasive gas, and the slightly heavier one to be a proposed element, <i><a href="/wiki/Coronium" title="Coronium">coronium</a></i>.
</p><p>Mendeleev devoted much study and made important contributions to the determination of the nature of such indefinite compounds as <a href="/wiki/Solution_(chemistry)" title="Solution (chemistry)">solutions</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mendeleyev_gold_Barry_Kent.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="371" data-file-width="364" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Mendeleyev_gold_Barry_Kent.JPG/170px-Mendeleyev_gold_Barry_Kent.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Mendeleyev_gold_Barry_Kent.JPG/255px-Mendeleyev_gold_Barry_Kent.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Mendeleyev_gold_Barry_Kent.JPG/340px-Mendeleyev_gold_Barry_Kent.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mendeleyev_gold_Barry_Kent.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mendeleev Medal</div></div></div>
<p>In another department of <a href="/wiki/Physical_chemistry" title="Physical chemistry">physical chemistry</a>, he investigated the expansion of liquids with heat, and devised a formula similar to <a href="/wiki/Gay-Lussac%27s_law" title="Gay-Lussac's law">Gay-Lussac's law</a> of the uniformity of the expansion of gases, while in 1861 he anticipated <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Andrews_(scientist)" title="Thomas Andrews (scientist)">Thomas Andrews</a>' conception of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_temperature" title="Critical temperature">critical temperature</a> of gases by defining the absolute boiling-point of a substance as the temperature at which cohesion and heat of vaporization become equal to zero and the liquid changes to vapor, irrespective of the pressure and volume.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB1911_54-3"><a href="#cite_note-EB1911-54">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mendeleev is given credit for the introduction of the <a href="/wiki/Metric_system" title="Metric system">metric system</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a>.
</p><p>He invented <i><a href="/wiki/Pyrocollodion" title="Pyrocollodion">pyrocollodion</a></i>, a kind of <a href="/wiki/Smokeless_powder" title="Smokeless powder">smokeless powder</a> based on <a href="/wiki/Nitrocellulose" title="Nitrocellulose">nitrocellulose</a>. This work had been commissioned by the Russian Navy, which however did not adopt its use. In 1892 Mendeleev organized its manufacture.
</p><p>Mendeleev studied petroleum origin and concluded hydrocarbons are abiogenic and form deep within the earth – see <a href="/wiki/Abiogenic_petroleum_origin" title="Abiogenic petroleum origin">Abiogenic petroleum origin</a>.
He wrote: "The capital fact to note is that petroleum was born in the depths of the earth, and it is only there that we must seek its origin." (Dmitri Mendeleev, 1877)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[57]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Activities_beyond_chemistry">Activities beyond chemistry</span></h2>
<p>Beginning in the 1870s, he published widely beyond chemistry, looking at aspects of Russian industry, and technical issues in agricultural productivity. He explored demographic issues, sponsored studies of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arctic_Sea" title="Arctic Sea">Arctic Sea</a>, tried to measure the efficacy of chemical fertilizers, and promoted the merchant navy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[58]</a></sup> He was especially active in improving the Russian petroleum industry, making detailed comparisons with the more advanced industry in <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[59]</a></sup> Although not well-grounded in economics, he had observed industry throughout his European travels, and in 1891 he helped convince the Ministry of Finance to impose temporary tariffs with the aim of fostering Russian <a href="/wiki/Infant_industry_argument" title="Infant industry argument">infant industries</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[60]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1890 he resigned his professorship at St. Petersburg University following a dispute with officials at the Ministry of Education over the treatment of university students.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[61]</a></sup> In 1892 he was appointed director of Russia's Central Bureau of Weights and Measures, and led the way to standardize fundamental prototypes and measurement procedures. He set up an inspection system, and introduced the metric system to Russia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[63]</a></sup>
</p><p>He debated against the scientific claims of spiritualism, arguing that metaphysical idealism was no more than ignorant superstition. He bemoaned the widespread acceptance of spiritualism in Russian culture, and its negative effects on the study of science.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vodka_myth">Vodka myth</span></h3>
<p>A very popular Russian story credits Mendeleev with setting the 40% standard strength of <a href="/wiki/Vodka" title="Vodka">vodka</a>. For example, <a href="/wiki/Russian_Standard_(vodka)" title="Russian Standard (vodka)">Russian Standard</a> vodka advertises: "In 1894, Dmitri Mendeleev, the greatest scientist in all Russia, received the decree to set the Imperial quality standard for Russian vodka and the 'Russian Standard' was born"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[65]</a></sup> Others cite "the highest quality of Russian vodka approved by the royal government commission headed by Mendeleev in 1894".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evseev2011_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Evseev2011-68">[66]</a></sup>
</p><p>In fact, the 40% standard was already introduced by the Russian government in 1843, when Mendeleev was nine years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evseev2011_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-Evseev2011-68">[66]</a></sup> It is true that Mendeleev in 1892 became head of the Archive of Weights and Measures in Saint Petersburg, and evolved it into a government bureau the following year, but that institution was charged with standardising Russian trade weights and measuring instruments, not setting any production quality standards, Also, Mendeleev's 1865 doctoral dissertation was entitled "A Discourse on the combination of alcohol and water", but it only discussed medical-strength alcohol concentrations over 70%, and he never wrote anything about vodka.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evseev2011_68-2"><a href="#cite_note-Evseev2011-68">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commemoration">Commemoration</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:X_50eca20c.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="453" data-file-width="604" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/X_50eca20c.jpg/220px-X_50eca20c.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/X_50eca20c.jpg/330px-X_50eca20c.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/X_50eca20c.jpg/440px-X_50eca20c.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:X_50eca20c.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Mendeleev in the city of <a href="/wiki/Mendeleyevsk" title="Mendeleyevsk">Mendeleyevsk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tatarstan" title="Tatarstan">Tatarstan</a></div></div></div>
<p>A number of places and objects are associated with the name and achievements of the scientist.
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a> his name was given to <a href="/wiki/D._I._Mendeleev_Institute_for_Metrology" title="D. I. Mendeleev Institute for Metrology">D. I. Mendeleev Institute for Metrology</a>, the National Metrology Institute,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[68]</a></sup> dealing with establishing and supporting national and worldwide standards for precise measurements. Next to it there is a monument to him that consists of his sitting statue and a depiction of his periodic table on the wall of the establishment.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Collegia" title="Twelve Collegia">Twelve Collegia</a> building, now being the centre of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg_State_University" title="Saint Petersburg State University">Saint Petersburg State University</a> and in Mendeleev's time – Head Pedagogical Institute – there is <a href="/wiki/Dmitry_Mendeleev%27s_Memorial_Museum_Apartment" title="Dmitry Mendeleev's Memorial Museum Apartment">Dmitry Mendeleev's Memorial Museum Apartment</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[69]</a></sup> with his archives. The street in front of these is named after him as <a href="/wiki/Lines_of_Vasilyevsky_Island#Mendeleyevskaya_liniya_(Mendeleyev_/_Mendeleev_Line)_and_Birzhevaya_liniya_(Stock_Exchange_Line)" title="Lines of Vasilyevsky Island">Mendeleevskaya liniya</a> (Mendeleev <a href="/wiki/Lines_of_Vasilyevsky_Island" title="Lines of Vasilyevsky Island">Line</a>).
</p><p>In Moscow, there is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mendeleev_Russian_University_of_Chemistry_and_Technology" title="Mendeleev Russian University of Chemistry and Technology">D. Mendeleyev University of Chemical Technology of Russia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[70]</a></sup>
</p><p>After him was also named <a href="/wiki/Mendelevium" title="Mendelevium">mendelevium</a>, which is a synthetic chemical element with the symbol Md (formerly Mv) and the atomic number 101. It is a metallic radioactive transuranic element in the actinide series, usually synthesized by bombarding einsteinium with alpha particles.
</p><p>The mineral mendeleevite-Ce, <span class="chemf nowrap">Cs<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>(Ce<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">22</sub></span>Ca<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>)(Si<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">70</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">175</sub></span>)(OH,F)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">14</sub></span>(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">21</sub></span></span>, was named in Mendeleev's honor in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[71]</a></sup> The related species mendeleevite-Nd, <span class="chemf nowrap">Cs<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">6</sub></span>[(Nd,REE)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">23</sub></span>Ca<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">7</sub></span>](Si<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">70</sub></span>O<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">175</sub></span>)(OH,F)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">19</sub></span>(H<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">2</sub></span>O)<span style="display:inline-block;margin-bottom:-0.3em;vertical-align:-0.4em;line-height:1em;font-size:80%;text-align:left"><sup style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline"></sup><br/><sub style="font-size:inherit;line-height:inherit;vertical-align:baseline">16</sub></span></span>, was described in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>A large lunar impact crater <a href="/wiki/Mendeleev_(crater)" title="Mendeleev (crater)">Mendeleev</a>, that is located on the far side of the Moon, also bears the name of the scientist.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Russian_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Russian Academy of Sciences">Russian Academy of Sciences</a> has occasionally awarded a Mendeleev Golden Medal since 1965.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[73]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In Mendeleev's day, his name was written <span title="Russian-language text"><span lang="ru">Дмитрій Ивановичъ Менделѣевъ</span></span>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">When the Princeton historian of science Michael Gordin reviewed this article as part of an analysis of the accuracy of Wikipedia for the 14 December 2005 issue of <i><a href="/wiki/Nature_(journal)" title="Nature (journal)">Nature</a></i>, he cited as one of Wikipedia's errors that "They say Mendeleev is the 14th child. He is the 14th surviving child of 17 total. 14 is right out." However in a January 2006 article in <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i>, it was noted that in Gordin's own 2004 biography of Mendeleev, he also had the Russian chemist listed as the 17th child, and quoted Gordin's response to this as being: "That's curious. I believe that is a typographical error in my book. Mendeleyev was the final child, that is certain, and the number the reliable sources have is 13." Gordin's book specifically says that Mendeleev's mother bore her husband "seventeen children, of whom eight survived to young adulthood", with Mendeleev being the youngest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[24]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[25]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

U.S. Atomic Energy Commission. Division of Technical Information. p. 25.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Atomic+Pioneers%3A+From+ancient+Greece+to+the+19th+century&amp;rft.pages=25&amp;rft.pub=U.S.+Atomic+Energy+Commission.+Division+of+Technical+Information&amp;rft.date=1975&amp;rft.aulast=Hiebert&amp;rft.aufirst=Ray+Eldon&amp;rft.au=Hiebert%2C+Roselyn&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADmitri+Mendeleev"></span></span>

<li id="cite_note-Gordin-23"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Gordin_23-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation book cs1" id="CITEREFGordin2004">Gordin, Michael D. (2004). <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/wellorderedthing00gord/page/229" rel="nofollow"><i>A Well-ordered Thing: Dmitrii Mendeleev and the Shadow of the Periodic Table</i></a>. Basic Books. pp. <a class="external text" href="https://archive.org/details/wellorderedthing00gord/page/229" rel="nofollow">229–230</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-465-02775-0" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-465-02775-0"><bdi>978-0-465-02775-0</bdi></a>. <q>Mendeleev seemed to have very few theological commitments. This was not for lack of exposure. His upbringing was actually heavily religious, and his mother – by far the dominating force in his youth – was exceptionally devout. One of his sisters even joined a fanatical religious sect for a time. Despite, or perhaps because of, this background, Mendeleev withheld comment on religious affairs for most of his life, reserving his few words for anti-clerical witticisms ... Mendeleev's son Ivan later vehemently denied claims that his father was devoutly Orthodox: "I have also heard the view of my father's 'church religiosity' – and I must reject this categorically. From his earliest years Father practically split from the church – and if he tolerated certain simple everyday rites, then only as an innocent national tradition, similar to Easter cakes, which he didn't consider worth fighting against." ... Mendeleev's opposition to traditional Orthodoxy was not due to either atheism or scientific materialism. Rather, he held to a form of romanticized deism.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=A+Well-ordered+Thing%3A+Dmitrii+Mendeleev+and+the+Shadow+of+the+Periodic+Table&amp;rft.pages=229-230&amp;rft.pub=Basic+Books&amp;rft.date=2004&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-465-02775-0&amp;rft.aulast=Gordin&amp;rft.aufirst=Michael+D.&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Farchive.org%2Fdetails%2Fwellorderedthing00gord%2Fpage%2F229&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADmitri+Mendeleev"></span></span>
</li>

Retrieved <span class="nowrap">14 March</span> 2011</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=The+New+York+Times&amp;rft.atitle=The+Nitpicking+of+the+Masses+vs.+the+Authority+of+the+Experts&amp;rft.date=2006-01-03&amp;rft.aulast=Johnson&amp;rft.aufirst=George&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.nytimes.com%2F2006%2F01%2F03%2Fscience%2F03comm.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ADmitri+Mendeleev"></span></span>
